nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span><sup>+</sup>)</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>A <b>proton</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particle</a>, symbol <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> or <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, with a positive <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> of +1<i>e</i> <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a> and a mass slightly less than that of a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>. Protons and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, each with masses of approximately one <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">atomic mass unit</a>, are collectively referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a>".\n</p><p>One or more protons are present in the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of every <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a>; they are a necessary part of the nucleus. The number of protons in the nucleus is the defining property of an element, and is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> (represented by the symbol <i>Z</i>). Since each <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> has a unique number of protons, each element has its own unique atomic number.\n</p><p>The word <i>proton</i> is Greek for "first", and this name was given to the hydrogen nucleus by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> in 1920. In previous years, Rutherford had discovered that the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> nucleus (known to be the lightest nucleus) could be extracted from the nuclei of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> by atomic collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Protons were therefore a candidate to be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_particle" title="Fundamental particle">fundamental particle</a>, and hence a building block of nitrogen and all other heavier atomic nuclei.\n</p><p>In the modern <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, protons are <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>, and like <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, the other <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a> (particles present in atomic nuclei), are composed of three <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>. Although protons were originally considered fundamental or <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a>, they are now known to be composed of three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_quark" title="Valence quark">valence quarks</a>: two <a href="/wiki/Up_quark" title="Up quark">up quarks</a> of charge +<span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">2</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">3</span></span><i>e</i> and one <a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down quark</a> of charge &#8211;<span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">3</span></span><i>e</i>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest masses</a> of quarks contribute only about 1% of a proton\'s mass, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mass_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mass-4">[4]</a></sup> The remainder of a proton\'s mass is due to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics_binding_energy" title="Quantum chromodynamics binding energy">quantum chromodynamics binding energy</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of the quarks and the energy of the <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluon</a> fields that bind the quarks together.  Because protons are not fundamental particles, they possess a physical size, though not a definite one; the root mean square <a href="/wiki/Charge_radius" title="Charge radius">charge radius</a> of a proton is about 0.84&#8211;0.87 <a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fm</a> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6984840000000000000&#9824;</span>0.84<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;15</sup></span> to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6984870000000000000&#9824;</span>0.87<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;15</sup>&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">m</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSI_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSI-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At sufficiently low temperatures, free protons will bind to <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>. However, the character of such bound protons does not change, and they remain protons. A fast proton moving through matter will slow by interactions with electrons and nuclei, until it is captured by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron cloud</a> of an atom. The result is a protonated atom, which is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> of hydrogen. In vacuum, when free electrons are present, a sufficiently slow proton may pick up a single free electron, becoming a neutral <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a>, which is chemically a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radical</a>. Such "free hydrogen atoms" tend to react chemically with many other types of atoms at sufficiently low energies. When free hydrogen atoms react with each other, they form neutral hydrogen molecules (H<sub>2</sub>), which are the most common molecular component of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_clouds" title="Molecular clouds">molecular clouds</a> in <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar space</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" Robert Oppenheimer">Oppenheimer</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Alexandru_Proca" title="Alexandru Proca">Proca</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Mills_Purcell" title="Edward Mills Purcell">Purcell</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Isidor_Isaac_Rabi" title="Isidor Isaac Rabi">Rabi</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Rutherford</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Soddy" title="Frederick Soddy">Soddy</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Strassmann</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3_Szil%C3%A1rd" title="Le&#243; Szil&#225;rd">Szil&#225;rd</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Teller" title="Edward Teller">Teller</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">Thomson</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Walton" title="Ernest Walton">Walton</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Wigner" title="Eugene Wigner">Wigner</a></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Nuclear_physics" title="Template:Nuclear physics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Nuclear_physics" title="Template talk:Nuclear physics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_physics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table style="float:right; width:30%; margin:0em 1em 1em 1em; background:#eee; border:#ccc solid">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_dropshade.png"><img alt="Question dropshade.png" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/20px-Question_dropshade.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/30px-Question_dropshade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/40px-Question_dropshade.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>\n<td><b>Unsolved problem in physics</b>:<br/><div style="padding:5px; font-style:italic;">How do the quarks and gluons carry the spin of protons?</div><small><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Protons are <a href="/wiki/Spin-%C2%BD" title="Spin-&#189;">spin-&#189;</a> <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> and are composed of three valence quarks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> making them <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryons</a> (a sub-type of <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>). The two <a href="/wiki/Up_quark" title="Up quark">up quarks</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down quark</a> of a proton are held together by the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong force</a>, mediated by <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cottingham_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cottingham-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>21&#8211;22</span></sup>A modern perspective has a proton composed of the valence quarks (up, up, down), the gluons, and transitory pairs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_quark" title="Sea quark">sea quarks</a>. Protons have a positive charge distribution which decays approximately exponentially, with a mean square <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> of about 0.8 fm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basdevant2_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basdevant2-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Protons and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> are both <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a>, which may be bound together by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a>. The nucleus of the most common <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a> (with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" title="Chemical symbol">chemical symbol</a> "H") is a lone proton. The nuclei of the heavy hydrogen isotopes <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> contain one proton bound to one and two neutrons, respectively. All other types of atomic nuclei are composed of two or more protons and various numbers of neutrons.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The concept of a hydrogen-like particle as a constituent of other atoms was developed over a long period. As early as 1815, <a href="/wiki/William_Prout" title="William Prout">William Prout</a> proposed that all atoms are composed of hydrogen atoms (which he called "protyles"), based on a simplistic interpretation of early values of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weights</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Prout%27s_hypothesis" title="Prout\'s hypothesis">Prout\'s hypothesis</a>), which was disproved when more accurate values were measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lecturer2006_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lecturer2006-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>39&#8211;42</span></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="212" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg/150px-Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg 1.5x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> at the first <a href="/wiki/Solvay_Conference" title="Solvay Conference">Solvay Conference</a>, 1911</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:82px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proton_detected_in_an_isopropanol_cloud_chamber.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="80" data-file-width="80" decoding="async" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Proton_detected_in_an_isopropanol_cloud_chamber.jpg" width="80"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proton_detected_in_an_isopropanol_cloud_chamber.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proton detected in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isopropanol" title="Isopropanol">isopropanol</a> <a href="/wiki/Cloud_chamber" title="Cloud chamber">cloud chamber</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1886, <a href="/wiki/Eugen_Goldstein" title="Eugen Goldstein">Eugen Goldstein</a> discovered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canal_rays" title="Canal rays">canal rays</a> (also known as anode rays) and showed that they were positively charged particles (ions) produced from gases. However, since particles from different gases had different values of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charge-to-mass_ratio" title="Charge-to-mass ratio">charge-to-mass ratio</a> (e/m), they could not be identified with a single particle, unlike the negative <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> discovered by <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Wien" title="Wilhelm Wien">Wilhelm Wien</a> in 1898 identified the hydrogen ion as particle with highest charge-to-mass ratio in ionized gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the discovery of the atomic nucleus by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> in 1911, <a href="/wiki/Antonius_van_den_Broek" title="Antonius van den Broek">Antonius van den Broek</a> proposed that the place of each element in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> (its atomic number) is equal to its nuclear charge. This was confirmed experimentally by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moseley" title="Henry Moseley">Henry Moseley</a> in 1913 using <a href="/wiki/X-ray_spectroscopy" title="X-ray spectroscopy">X-ray spectra</a>.\n</p><p>In 1917 (in experiments reported in 1919), Rutherford proved that the hydrogen nucleus is present in other nuclei, a result usually described as the discovery of protons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Rutherford had earlier learned to produce hydrogen nuclei as a type of radiation produced as a product of the impact of alpha particles on nitrogen gas, and recognize them by their unique penetration signature in air and their appearance in scintillation detectors. These experiments were begun when Rutherford had noticed that, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_particles" title="Alpha particles">alpha particles</a> were shot into air (mostly nitrogen), his scintillation detectors showed the signatures of typical hydrogen nuclei as a product. After experimentation Rutherford traced the reaction to the nitrogen in air, and found that when alphas were produced into pure nitrogen gas, the effect was larger. Rutherford determined that this hydrogen could have come only from the nitrogen, and therefore nitrogen must contain hydrogen nuclei. One hydrogen nucleus was being knocked off by the impact of the alpha particle, producing oxygen-17 in the process. This was the first reported <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reaction</a>, <sup>14</sup>N + &#945; &#8594; <sup>17</sup>O + p. (This reaction would later be observed happening directly in a cloud chamber in 1925).\n</p><p>Rutherford knew hydrogen to be the simplest and lightest element and was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Prout%27s_hypothesis" title="Prout\'s hypothesis">Prout\'s hypothesis</a> that hydrogen was the building block of all elements. Discovery that the hydrogen nucleus is present in all other nuclei as an elementary particle led Rutherford to give the hydrogen nucleus a special name as a particle, since he suspected that hydrogen, the lightest element, contained only one of these particles. He named this new fundamental building block of the nucleus the <i>proton,</i> after the neuter singular of the Greek word for "first", &#960;&#961;&#8182;&#964;&#959;&#957;. However, Rutherford also had in mind the word <i>protyle</i> as used by Prout. Rutherford spoke at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="British Association for the Advancement of Science">British Association for the Advancement of Science</a> at its <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a> meeting beginning 24 August 1920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Rutherford was asked by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Lodge" title="Oliver Lodge">Oliver Lodge</a> for a new name for the positive hydrogen nucleus to avoid confusion with the neutral hydrogen atom. He initially suggested both <i>proton</i> and <i>prouton</i> (after Prout).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Rutherford later reported that the meeting had accepted his suggestion that the hydrogen nucleus be named the "proton", following Prout\'s word "protyle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The first use of the word "proton" in the scientific literature appeared in 1920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recent research has shown that thunderstorms can produce protons with energies of up to several tens of MeV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KohnEbert_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-KohnEbert-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KohnHarakeh_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-KohnHarakeh-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Protons are routinely used for accelerators for <a href="/wiki/Proton_therapy" title="Proton therapy">proton therapy</a> or various <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> experiments, with the most powerful example being the <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a>.\n</p><p>In a July 2017 paper, researchers measured the mass of a proton to be <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000100727646658300&#9824;</span>1.007<span style="margin-left:.25em;">276</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">466</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">583</span><span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+15<br/>&#8722;29</span></span>&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_units" title="Atomic mass units">atomic mass units</a></span> (the values in parentheses being the statistical and systematic uncertainties, respectively), which is lower than measurements from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CODATA_2014" title="CODATA 2014">CODATA 2014</a> value by three standard deviations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stability">Stability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Stability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">Proton decay</a></div>\n<table style="float:right; width:30%; margin:0em 1em 1em 1em; background:#eee; border:#ccc solid">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_dropshade.png"><img alt="Question dropshade.png" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/20px-Question_dropshade.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/30px-Question_dropshade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/40px-Question_dropshade.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>\n<td><b>Unsolved problem in physics</b>:<br/><div style="padding:5px; font-style:italic;">Are protons fundamentally stable? Or do they decay with a finite lifetime as predicted by some extensions to the standard model?</div><small><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The free proton (a proton not bound to nucleons or electrons) is a stable particle that has not been observed to break down spontaneously to other particles. Free protons are found naturally in a number of situations in which energies or temperatures are high enough to separate them from electrons, for which they have some affinity. Free protons exist in <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasmas</a> in which temperatures are too high to allow them to combine with <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>. Free protons of high energy and velocity make up 90% of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, which propagate in vacuum for interstellar distances. Free protons are <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">emitted directly</a> from <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> in some rare types of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>. Protons also result (along with electrons and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antineutrino" title="Antineutrino">antineutrinos</a>) from the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of free neutrons, which are unstable.\n</p><p>The spontaneous decay of free protons has never been observed, and protons are therefore considered stable particles according to the Standard Model. However, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_unified_theory" title="Grand unified theory">grand unified theories</a> (GUTs) of particle physics predict that <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">proton decay</a> should take place with lifetimes between 10<sup>31</sup> to 10<sup>36</sup> years and experimental searches have established lower bounds on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> of a proton for various assumed decay products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Experiments at the <a href="/wiki/Super-Kamiokande" title="Super-Kamiokande">Super-Kamiokande</a> detector in Japan gave lower limits for proton <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7041208280159999999&#9824;</span>6.6<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>33</sup>&#160;years</span> for decay to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antimuon" title="Antimuon">antimuon</a> and a neutral <a href="/wiki/Pion" title="Pion">pion</a>, and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7041258772319999999&#9824;</span>8.2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>33</sup>&#160;years</span> for decay to a <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> and a neutral pion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\nAnother experiment at the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">Sudbury Neutrino Observatory</a> in Canada searched for <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> resulting from residual nuclei resulting from the decay of a proton from oxygen-16. This experiment was designed to detect decay to any product, and established a lower limit to a proton lifetime of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7036662709600000000&#9824;</span>2.1<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>29</sup>&#160;years</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, protons are known to transform into <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> through the process of <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> (also called <a href="/wiki/Inverse_beta_decay" title="Inverse beta decay">inverse beta decay</a>). For free protons, this process does not occur spontaneously but only when energy is supplied. The equation is:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#8722;</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> &#8594; <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> + <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></a></dd></dl>\n<p>The process is reversible; neutrons can convert back to protons through <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, a common form of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>. In fact, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_neutron" title="Free neutron">free neutron</a> decays this way, with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> of about 15 minutes.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quarks_and_the_mass_of_a_proton">Quarks and the mass of a proton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Quarks and the mass of a proton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">quantum chromodynamics</a>, the modern theory of the nuclear force, most of the mass of protons and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> is explained by <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>. The mass of a proton is about 80&#8211;100 times greater than the sum of the rest masses of the <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> that make it up, while the <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a> have zero rest mass. The extra energy of the <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a> in a region within a proton, as compared to the rest energy of the quarks alone in the <a href="/wiki/QCD_vacuum" title="QCD vacuum">QCD vacuum</a>, accounts for almost 99% of the mass. The rest mass of a proton is, thus, the <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">invariant mass</a> of the system of moving quarks and gluons that make up the particle, and, in such systems, even the energy of massless particles is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence#Fast-moving_objects_and_systems_of_objects" title="Mass-energy equivalence">still measured</a> as part of the rest mass of the system.\n</p><p>Two terms are used in referring to the mass of the quarks that make up protons: <i><a href="/wiki/Current_quark" title="Current quark">current quark</a> mass</i> refers to the mass of a quark by itself, while <i><a href="/wiki/Constituent_quark" title="Constituent quark">constituent quark</a> mass</i> refers to the current quark mass plus the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluon</a> <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">particle field</a> surrounding the quark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>285&#8211;286</span></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2003_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2003-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>150&#8211;151</span></sup> These masses typically have very different values. As noted, most of a proton\'s mass comes from the gluons that bind the current quarks together, rather than from the quarks themselves. While gluons are inherently massless, they possess energy&#8212;to be more specific, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics_binding_energy" title="Quantum chromodynamics binding energy">quantum chromodynamics binding energy</a> (QCBE)&#8212;and it is this that contributes so greatly to the overall mass of protons (see <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">mass in special relativity</a>). A proton has a mass of approximately 938&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt#As_a_unit_of_mass" title="Electron volt">MeV/c<sup>2</sup></a>, of which the rest mass of its three valence quarks contributes only about 9.4&#160;MeV/c<sup>2</sup>; much of the remainder can be attributed to the gluons\' <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics_binding_energy" title="Quantum chromodynamics binding energy">QCBE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The internal dynamics of protons are complicated, because they are determined by the quarks\' exchanging gluons, and interacting with various vacuum condensates. <a href="/wiki/Lattice_QCD" title="Lattice QCD">Lattice QCD</a> provides a way of calculating the mass of a proton directly from the theory to any accuracy, in principle. The most recent calculations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fodor_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fodor-32">[32]</a></sup> claim that the mass is determined to better than 4% accuracy, even to 1% accuracy (see Figure S5 in D&#252;rr <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fodor_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fodor-32">[32]</a></sup>). These claims are still controversial, because the calculations cannot yet be done with quarks as light as they are in the real world. This means that the predictions are found by a process of <a href="/wiki/Extrapolation" title="Extrapolation">extrapolation</a>, which can introduce systematic errors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perdrisat_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perdrisat-33">[33]</a></sup> It is hard to tell whether these errors are controlled properly, because the quantities that are compared to experiment are the masses of the <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>, which are known in advance.\n</p><p>These recent calculations are performed by massive supercomputers, and, as noted by Boffi and Pasquini: "a detailed description of the nucleon structure is still missing because ... long-distance behavior requires a nonperturbative and/or numerical treatment..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boffi_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boffi-34">[34]</a></sup>\nMore conceptual approaches to the structure of protons are: the <a href="/wiki/Skyrmion" title="Skyrmion">topological soliton</a> approach originally due to <a href="/wiki/Tony_Skyrme" title="Tony Skyrme">Tony Skyrme</a> and the more accurate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AdS/QCD" title="AdS/QCD">AdS/QCD approach</a> that extends it to include a <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a> of gluons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> various QCD-inspired models like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bag_model" title="Bag model">bag model</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Constituent_quark" title="Constituent quark">constituent quark</a> model, which were popular in the 1980s, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SVZ_sum_rules" title="SVZ sum rules">SVZ sum rules</a>, which allow for rough approximate mass calculations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> These methods do not have the same accuracy as the more brute-force lattice QCD methods, at least not yet.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charge_radius">Charge radius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Charge radius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table style="float:right; width:30%; margin:0em 1em 1em 1em; background:#eee; border:#ccc solid">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_dropshade.png"><img alt="Question dropshade.png" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/20px-Question_dropshade.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/30px-Question_dropshade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/40px-Question_dropshade.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>\n<td><b>Unsolved problem in physics</b>:<br/><div style="padding:5px; font-style:italic;">What is the true charge radius of a proton?</div><small><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proton_radius_puzzle" title="Proton radius puzzle">Proton radius puzzle</a></div>\n<p>The problem of defining a radius for an atomic nucleus (proton) is similar to the problem of <a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">atomic radius</a>, in that neither atoms nor their nuclei have definite boundaries. However, the nucleus can be modeled as a sphere of positive charge for the interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Electron_scattering" title="Electron scattering">electron scattering</a> experiments: because there is no definite boundary to the nucleus, the electrons "see" a range of cross-sections, for which a mean can be taken. The qualification of "rms" (for "<a href="/wiki/Root_mean_square" title="Root mean square">root mean square</a>") arises because it is the nuclear cross-section, proportional to the square of the radius, which is determining for electron scattering.\n</p><p>The internationally accepted value of a proton\'s <a href="/wiki/Charge_radius" title="Charge radius">charge radius</a> is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6984876800000000000&#9824;</span>0.8768&#160;<a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fm</a></span> (see <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(length)#1E-15" title="Orders of magnitude (length)">orders of magnitude</a> for comparison to other sizes). This value is based on measurements involving a proton and an electron (namely, <a href="/wiki/Electron_scattering" title="Electron scattering">electron scattering</a> measurements and complex calculation involving scattering cross section based on <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Rosenbluth" title="Marshall Rosenbluth">Rosenbluth</a> equation for <a href="/wiki/Momentum-transfer_cross_section" title="Momentum-transfer cross section">momentum-transfer cross section</a>), and studies of the atomic <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy levels</a> of hydrogen and deuterium.\n</p><p>However, in 2010 an international research team published a proton charge radius measurement via the <a href="/wiki/Lamb_shift" title="Lamb shift">Lamb shift</a> in muonic hydrogen (an <a href="/wiki/Exotic_atom" title="Exotic atom">exotic atom</a> made of a proton and a negatively charged <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muon</a>). As a muon is 200 times heavier than an electron, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Broglie_wavelength" title="De Broglie wavelength">de Broglie wavelength</a> is correspondingly shorter.  This smaller <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbital</a> is much more sensitive to the proton\'s charge radius, so allows more precise measurement.  Their measurement of the <a href="/wiki/Root_mean_square" title="Root mean square">root-mean-square</a> charge radius of a proton is "<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6984841840000000000&#9824;</span>0.841<span style="margin-left:.25em;">84</span>(67)&#160;fm</span>, which differs by 5.0 <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviations</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CODATA" title="CODATA">CODATA</a> value of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6984876800000000000&#9824;</span>0.8768(69)&#160;fm</span>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radius_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radius-37">[37]</a></sup> In January 2013, an updated value for the charge radius of a proton&#8212;<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6984840870000000000&#9824;</span>0.840<span style="margin-left:.25em;">87</span>(39)&#160;fm</span>&#8212;was published. The precision was improved by 1.7 times, increasing the significance of the discrepancy to 7&#963;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chargeradius_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chargeradius-38">[38]</a></sup>\nThe 2014 CODATA adjustment slightly reduced the recommended value for the proton radius (computed using electron measurements only) to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6984875100000000000&#9824;</span>0.8751(61)&#160;fm</span>, but this leaves the discrepancy at 5.6&#963;.\n</p><p>The international research team that obtained this result at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_Scherrer_Institut" title="Paul Scherrer Institut">Paul Scherrer Institut</a> in <a href="/wiki/Villigen" title="Villigen">Villigen</a> includes scientists from the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute_of_Quantum_Optics" title="Max Planck Institute of Quantum Optics">Max Planck Institute of Quantum Optics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludwig-Maximilians-Universit%C3%A4t" title="Ludwig-Maximilians-Universit&#228;t">Ludwig-Maximilians-Universit&#228;t</a>, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Institut_f%C3%BCr_Strahlwerkzeuge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Institut f&#252;r Strahlwerkzeuge (page does not exist)">Institut f&#252;r Strahlwerkzeuge</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universit%C3%A4t_Stuttgart" title="Universit&#228;t Stuttgart">Universit&#228;t Stuttgart</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Coimbra" title="University of Coimbra">University of Coimbra</a>, Portugal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nanotechnology_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-nanotechnology-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>  The team is now attempting to explain the discrepancy, and re-examining the results of both previous high-precision measurements and complex calculations involving <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scattering</a> <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">cross section</a>. If no errors are found in the measurements or calculations, it could be necessary to re-examine the world\'s most precise and best-tested fundamental theory: <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nanotechnology_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-nanotechnology-39">[39]</a></sup> The proton radius remains a puzzle as of 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Perhaps the discrepancy is due to new physics, or the explanation may be an ordinary physics effect that has been missed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Proton_Radius_Puzzle_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Proton_Radius_Puzzle-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The radius is linked to the form factor and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Momentum_transfer_cross_section" title="Momentum transfer cross section">momentum transfer cross section</a>. The atomic form factor G modifies the cross section corresponding to point-like proton.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}R_{e}^{2}&amp;=-6{{\\frac {dG_{e}}{dq^{2}}}\\,{\\Bigg \\vert }\\,}_{q^{2}=0}\\\\{\\frac {d\\sigma }{d\\Omega }}\\ &amp;={{\\frac {d\\sigma }{d\\Omega }}\\,{\\Bigg \\vert }\\,}_{point}G^{2}(q^{2})\\end{aligned}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n</mtd>\n<mtd>\n<mi></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; width:24.14ex; height:13.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_form_factor" title="Atomic form factor">atomic form factor</a> is related to the wave function density of the target:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle G(q^{2})=\\int e^{iqr}\\psi (r)^{2}dr^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>q</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8747;<!-- &#8747; width:23.574ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The form factor can be split in electric and magnetic form factors. These can be further written as linear combinations of Dirac and Pauli form factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Proton_Radius_Puzzle_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Proton_Radius_Puzzle-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}G_{m}&amp;=F_{D}+F_{P}\\\\G_{e}&amp;=F_{D}-\\tau F_{P}\\\\{\\frac {d\\sigma }{d\\Omega }}&amp;={{\\frac {d\\sigma }{d\\Omega }}\\,{\\Bigg \\vert }\\,}_{NS}{\\frac {1}{1+\\tau }}\\left(G_{e}^{2}\\left(q^{2}\\right)+{\\frac {\\tau }{\\epsilon }}G_{m}^{2}\\left(q^{2}\\right)\\right)\\end{aligned}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>m</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n<mtd>\n<mi></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>F</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>F</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>P</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n<mtd>\n<mi></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>F</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo maxsize="2.470em" minsize="2.470em">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mi>S</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>&#964;<!-- &#964; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>q</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>&#964;<!-- &#964; --></mi>\n<mi>&#1013;<!-- &#1013; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>G</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>m</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msubsup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>q</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}G_{m}&amp;=F_{D}+F_{P}\\\\G_{e}&amp;=F_{D}-\\tau F_{P}\\\\{\\frac {d\\sigma }{d\\Omega }}&amp;={{\\frac {d\\sigma }{d\\Omega }}\\,{\\Bigg \\vert }\\,}_{NS}{\\frac {1}{1+\\tau }}\\left(G_{e}^{2}\\left(q^{2}\\right)+{\\frac {\\tau }{\\epsilon }}G_{m}^{2}\\left(q^{2}\\right)\\right)\\end{aligned}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}G_{m}&amp;=F_{D}+F_{P}\\\\G_{e}&amp;=F_{D}-\\tau F_{P}\\\\{\\frac {d\\sigma }{d\\Omega }}&amp;={{\\frac {d\\sigma }{d\\Omega }}\\,{\\Bigg \\vert }\\,}_{NS}{\\frac {1}{1+\\tau }}\\left(G_{e}^{2}\\left(q^{2}\\right)+{\\frac {\\tau }{\\epsilon }}G_{m}^{2}\\left(q^{2}\\right)\\right)\\end{aligned}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b56e2f5ec747d8341e44687af82a444c38d6a2dd" style="vertical-align: -5.367ex; margin-bottom: -0.304ex; width:45.249ex; height:12.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pressure_inside_the_proton">Pressure inside the proton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Pressure inside the proton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since the proton is composed of quarks confined by gluons, an equivalent <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> which acts on the quarks can be defined. This allows calculation of their distribution as a function of distance from the centre using <a href="/wiki/Compton_scattering" title="Compton scattering">Compton scattering</a> of high-energy electrons (DVCS, for <i>deeply virtual Compton scattering</i>). The pressure is maximum at the centre, about 10<sup>35</sup> Pa which is greater than the pressure inside a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> It is positive (repulsive) to a radial distance of about 0.6 fm, negative (attractive) at greater distances, and very weak beyond about 2 fm.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Charge_radius_in_solvated_proton.2C_hydronium"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Charge_radius_in_solvated_proton,_hydronium">Charge radius in solvated proton, hydronium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Charge radius in solvated proton, hydronium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The radius of hydrated proton appears in the <a href="/wiki/Born_equation" title="Born equation">Born equation</a> for calculating the hydration enthalpy of <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction_of_free_protons_with_ordinary_matter">Interaction of free protons with ordinary matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Interaction of free protons with ordinary matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Although protons have affinity for oppositely charged electrons, this is a relatively low-energy interaction and so free protons must lose sufficient velocity (and <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>) in order to become closely associated and bound to electrons. High energy protons, in traversing ordinary matter, lose energy by collisions with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_nuclei" title="Atomic nuclei">atomic nuclei</a>, and by <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionization</a> of atoms (removing electrons) until they are slowed sufficiently to be captured by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron cloud</a> in a normal atom.\n</p><p>However, in such an association with an electron, the character of the bound proton is not changed, and it remains a proton. The attraction of low-energy free protons to any electrons present in normal matter (such as the electrons in normal atoms) causes free protons to stop and to form a new chemical bond with an atom. Such a bond happens at any sufficiently "cold" temperature (i.e., comparable to temperatures at the surface of the Sun) and with any type of atom. Thus, in interaction with any type of normal (non-plasma) matter, low-velocity free protons are attracted to electrons in any atom or molecule with which they come in contact, causing the proton and molecule to combine. Such molecules are then said to be "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protonated" title="Protonated">protonated</a>", and chemically they often, as a result, become so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted_acid" title="Br&#248;nsted acid">Br&#248;nsted acids</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proton_in_chemistry">Proton in chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Proton in chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_number">Atomic number</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Atomic number">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, the number of protons in the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of an atom is known as the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>, which determines the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> to which the atom belongs. For example, the atomic number of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> is 17; this means that each chlorine atom has 17 protons and that all atoms with 17 protons are chlorine atoms. The chemical properties of each atom are determined by the number of (negatively charged) <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, which for neutral atoms is equal to the number of (positive) protons so that the total charge is zero. For example, a neutral chlorine atom has 17 protons and 17 electrons, whereas a Cl<sup>&#8722;</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anion</a> has 17 protons and 18 electrons for a total charge of &#8722;1.\n</p><p>All atoms of a given element are not necessarily identical, however, as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Number_of_neutrons" title="Number of neutrons">number of neutrons</a> may vary to form different <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, and energy levels may differ forming different <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a>. For example, there are two stable <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_chlorine" title="Isotopes of chlorine">isotopes of chlorine</a>: <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">35</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">17</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> with 35 &#8722; 17 = 18 neutrons and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">37</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">17</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> with 37 &#8722; 17 = 20 neutrons.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_ion">Hydrogen ion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hydrogen ion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hydron_(chemistry)" title="Hydron (chemistry)">Hydron (chemistry)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="230" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hydrogen.svg/220px-Hydrogen.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hydrogen.svg/330px-Hydrogen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hydrogen.svg/440px-Hydrogen.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protium, the most common isotope of hydrogen, consists of one proton and one electron (it has no neutrons). The term "hydrogen ion" (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) implies that that H-atom has lost its one electron, causing only a proton to remain. Thus, in chemistry, the terms "proton" and "hydrogen ion" (for the protium isotope) are used synonymously</div></div></div>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:20%; ;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8220; ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8221; ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">The proton is a unique chemical species, being a bare nucleus. As a consequence it has no independent existence in the condensed state and is invariably found bound by a pair of electrons to another atom.</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">Ross Stewart, <i>The Proton: Application to Organic Chemistry</i> (1985, p. 1)</cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>In chemistry, the term proton refers to the hydrogen ion, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. Since the atomic number of hydrogen is 1, a hydrogen ion has no electrons and corresponds to a bare nucleus, consisting of a proton (and 0 neutrons for the most abundant isotope <i>protium</i> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-1" title="Hydrogen-1"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>). The proton is a "bare charge" with only about 1/64,000 of the radius of a hydrogen atom, and so is extremely reactive chemically. The free proton, thus, has an extremely short lifetime in chemical systems such as liquids and it reacts immediately with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron cloud</a> of any available molecule. In aqueous solution, it forms the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydronium_ion" title="Hydronium ion">hydronium ion</a>, H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>, which in turn is further <a href="/wiki/Solvation" title="Solvation">solvated</a> by water molecules in <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_ion#Cation_(positively_charged)" title="Hydrogen ion">clusters</a> such as [H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup> and [H<sub>9</sub>O<sub>4</sub>]<sup>+</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Headrick_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Headrick-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The transfer of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> in an <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="Br&#248;nsted&#8211;Lowry acid&#8211;base theory">acid&#8211;base reaction</a> is usually referred to as "proton transfer". The <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> is referred to as a proton donor and the <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> as a proton acceptor. Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemical</a> terms such as <a href="/wiki/Proton_pump" title="Proton pump">proton pump</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_channel" title="Proton channel">proton channel</a> refer to the movement of hydrated <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions.\n</p><p>The ion produced by removing the electron from a <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> atom is known as a deuteron, not a proton. Likewise, removing an electron from a <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> atom produces a triton.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Proton_nuclear_magnetic_resonance_.28NMR.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Proton_nuclear_magnetic_resonance_(NMR)">Proton nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Proton nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Also in chemistry, the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_NMR" title="Proton NMR">proton NMR</a>" refers to the observation of hydrogen-1 nuclei in (mostly <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic</a>) molecules by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">nuclear magnetic resonance</a>. This method uses the <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> of the proton, which has the value one-half. The name refers to examination of protons as they occur in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-1" title="Hydrogen-1">protium</a> (hydrogen-1 atoms) in compounds, and does not imply that free protons exist in the compound being studied.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_exposure">Human exposure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Human exposure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_body" title="Effect of spaceflight on the body">Effect of spaceflight on the body</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Proton_therapy" title="Proton therapy">Proton therapy</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiments_Package" title="Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package">Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Packages</a> (ALSEP) determined that more than 95% of the particles in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> are electrons and protons, in approximately equal numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPI_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-LPI-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNL_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-BNL-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Because the Solar Wind <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">Spectrometer</a> made continuous measurements, it was possible to measure how the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">Earth\'s magnetic field</a> affects arriving solar wind particles. For about two-thirds of each orbit, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> is outside of the Earth\'s magnetic field. At these times, a typical proton density was 10 to 20 per cubic centimeter, with most protons having velocities between 400 and 650 kilometers per second. For about five days of each month, the Moon is inside the Earth\'s geomagnetic tail, and typically no solar wind particles were detectable. For the remainder of each lunar orbit, the Moon is in a transitional region known as the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosheath" title="Magnetosheath">magnetosheath</a>, where the Earth\'s magnetic field affects the solar wind but does not completely exclude it. In this region, the particle flux is reduced, with typical proton velocities of 250 to 450 kilometers per second. During the lunar night, the spectrometer was shielded from the solar wind by the Moon and no solar wind particles were measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPI_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-LPI-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Protons also have extrasolar origin from galactic <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, where they make up about 90% of the total particle flux. These protons often have higher energy than solar wind protons, and their intensity is far more uniform and less variable than protons coming from the Sun, the production of which is heavily affected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_proton_event" title="Solar proton event">solar proton events</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">coronal mass ejections</a>.\n</p><p>Research has been performed on the dose-rate effects of protons, as typically found in <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">space travel</a>, on human health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNL_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-BNL-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biblioteca_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-biblioteca-47">[47]</a></sup> To be more specific, there are hopes to identify what specific chromosomes are damaged, and to define the damage, during <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> development from proton exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNL_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-BNL-46">[46]</a></sup> Another study looks into determining "the effects of exposure to proton irradiation on neurochemical and behavioral endpoints, including <a href="/wiki/Dopaminergic" title="Dopaminergic">dopaminergic</a> functioning, <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>-induced conditioned taste aversion learning, and spatial learning and memory as measured by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morris_water_maze" title="Morris water maze">Morris water maze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biblioteca_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-biblioteca-47">[47]</a></sup> Electrical charging of a spacecraft due to interplanetary proton bombardment has also been proposed for study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> There are many more studies that pertain to space travel, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_cosmic_rays" title="Galactic cosmic rays">galactic cosmic rays</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Health_threat_from_cosmic_rays" title="Health threat from cosmic rays">possible health effects</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_proton_event" title="Solar proton event">solar proton event</a> exposure.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/STS-65#Mission_parameters" title="STS-65">American Biostack and Soviet Biorack</a> space travel experiments have demonstrated the severity of molecular damage induced by heavy ions on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micro_organism" title="Micro organism">micro organisms</a> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemia" title="Artemia">Artemia</a> cysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planel_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planel-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiproton">Antiproton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Antiproton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">Antiproton</a></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CPT-symmetry" title="CPT-symmetry">CPT-symmetry</a> puts strong constraints on the relative properties of particles and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiparticles" title="Antiparticles">antiparticles</a> and, therefore, is open to stringent tests. For example, the charges of a proton and antiproton must sum to exactly zero. This equality has been tested to one part in <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>8</sup></span>. The equality of their masses has also been tested to better than one part in <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>8</sup></span>. By holding antiprotons in a <a href="/wiki/Penning_trap" title="Penning trap">Penning trap</a>, the equality of the charge-to-mass ratio of protons and antiprotons has been tested to one part in <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009600000000000000&#9824;</span>6<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>9</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">magnetic moment</a> of antiprotons has been measured with error of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6997800000000000000&#9824;</span>8<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;3</sup></span> nuclear <a href="/wiki/Bohr_magneton" title="Bohr magneton">Bohr magnetons</a>, and is found to be equal and opposite to that of a proton.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul
